+0000","feedId":21,"bgimg":"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/The-Mystery-of-DB-Cooper-trailer-700x300.jpg","linkMd5":"4c7129afeb7b5e300e56c925a30cd793","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn38@2020_5/2020/11/12/04-49-51-561_17bb4ae7adcb5a28.webp","destWidth":700,"destHeight":300,"sourceBytes":37960,"destBytes":24206,"author":"Ben Pearson","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/The-Mystery-of-DB-Cooper-trailer-700x300.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn38@2020_5/2020/11/12/04-49-51-561_17bb4ae7adcb5a28.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/slashfilm?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn23@2020_1/2020/11/12/04-50-25-710_9a442e8760d02cd4.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/slashfilm?i=i0GNxGDYN3c:CjFRyPjGgqc:V_sGLiPBpWU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn95@2020_3/2020/11/12/04-50-25-734_23efadfe8847042d.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/slashfilm?d=I9og5sOYxJI":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn81@2020_5/2020/11/12/04-50-27-215_879c5505f034c3d2.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/slashfilm?i=i0GNxGDYN3c:CjFRyPjGgqc:EGzC5_Ws_ZE":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx22/cdn74@2020_6/2020/11/12/04-50-26-795_b4c29f83ac8be8d0.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/slashfilm/~4/i0GNxGDYN3c":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn65@2020_6/2020/11/12/04-50-26-505_10a9db1cd67a3f4d.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1605156575082},{"createdTime":"2020-11-12 12:49:35","updatedTime":"2020-11-12 12:49:35","title":"‘Mank’: It’s Once Again Time to Point Out David Fincher Shoots Many Takes","link":"https://www.slashfilm.com/?p=645352","description":"<p><a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/david-fincher-mank-takes.jpg\"><img class=\"aligncenter size-medium wp-image-645359\" src=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/david-fincher-mank-takes-700x321.jpg\" alt=\"david fincher mank takes\" width=\"700\" height=\"321\" srcset=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/david-fincher-mank-takes-700x321.jpg 700w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/david-fincher-mank-takes-360x165.jpg 360w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/david-fincher-mank-takes-768x352.jpg 768w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/david-fincher-mank-takes.jpg 948w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px\" /></a></p>\n<p>You know the drill: anytime there&#8217;s a new <strong>David Fincher</strong> movie we are all required by law to talk about how many takes he shot. Cast members of the film in question come forward and say, &#8220;I shot a lot of takes!&#8221; We report it. Fincher rolls his eyes. Then we move on, only to revisit the story again whenever Fincher gets around to making another movie. After taking a hiatus from feature films, Fincher is back this year with <strong><em>Mank</em></strong>, and sure enough, stories are starting to trickle in about Fincher and all his damn scene takes.</p>\n<p><span id=\"more-645352\"></span></p>\n<p>We&#8217;ve been here before. Over the summer, I wrote up a <a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/mank-takes/\">story</a> about <em>Mank</em> cast member <strong>Amanda Seyfried</strong> claiming that one of her scenes required something like 200 takes and about an entire week to film. &#8220;I was part of scenes with tons of people in it and we would do it for an entire week,&#8221; Seyfried said. &#8220;I can’t tell you how many takes we did, but I would guess 200, maybe I could be wrong and could be way off. Um, I could be underestimating by five days of one scene when I didn’t have one line… &#8216;You think I can just relax?&#8217; No, because there are probably about nine or 10 different camera angles that had been on me at one point.&#8221;</p>\n<p>To be clear: there&#8217;s a good chance Seyfried is exaggerating a bit. But multiple Fincher takes have become a gold standard at this point. And a new issue of <a href=\"https://www.gamesradar.com/mank-cast-on-working-with-david-fincher-it-does-feel-like-groundhog-day/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"nofollow noopener\">Total Film magazine</a> has more people involved with <em>Mank</em> talking about Fincher and his many, many, many takes. &#8220;We did take after take after take after take,&#8221; said <strong>Charles Dance</strong>, who plays William Randolph Hearst in the film. &#8220;And [<em>Mank </em>star <strong>Gary Oldman</strong>] said to David at one point, &#8216;David, I’ve done this scene a hundred fucking times.&#8217; And Fincher said, &#8216;Yeah, I know, but this is 101. Reset!'&#8221;</p>\n<p>In the same issue, Fincher doesn&#8217;t deny this. &#8220;I&#8217;m fairly didactic about, &#8216;These are the things that the scene needs to accomplish for me, and we will continue to play, to look for ways to underline these ideas that are as subtle as we can make them,'&#8221; the director said, adding:</p>\n<blockquote><p>&#8220;It’s a hard thing to say to actors, ‘I want a cohesive, great performance in the master [shot].&#8217; And then, &#8216;I want a cohesive, great performance in the alternate master.’ And then, &#8216;I want a cohesive, great performance in the over-the-shoulder. And I want a cohesive, great performance in his over-the-shoulder on to you. And I want the singles.&#8217; Because I don’t want to cut a scene based on where you are at personally on Tuesday. I don’t think I could go into the edit room knowing that I was going to have to cut around somebody who didn’t deliver. Part of it is you cast really great people and get the fuck out of the way.&#8221;</p></blockquote>\n<p>Fincher has addressed this sort of thing before. &#8220;I like the idea that the first three takes, you&#8217;re just rehearsing,&#8221; he said in an interview <a href=\"https://www.theguardian.com/film/2009/feb/03/david-fincher-interview-transcript\">back in 2009</a>, and when the interviewer pointed out that Fincher was notorious for his many takes, the filmmaker countered: &#8220;This is bullshit. Look, you&#8217;re spending $150m, unbelievable amounts of money to ship period vehicles from Illinois down to Louisiana and get them working. There are teams of people making these cars work, all this stuff. So you get there and you&#8217;re going to shoot three takes and then go home?&#8221;</p>\n<p>He also added: &#8220;Some [actors] resent it and go, &#8216;My best stuff was when I had a lot of energy after my mochaccino and now my energy&#8217;s gone,&#8217; but a lot of actors work it out in their heads, they figure it out and have an idea of what they&#8217;re going to do. I can see that and I like to move past that, to where they&#8217;ve forgotten why they came, or who they are. And it is about choreography, where the eye of the audience finds that person and that person is revealed and they come forward and say their line. All those things in concert. So, you spend all that money to get there, so you might as well make sure you got it.&#8221;</p>\n<p>In other words: Fincher is going to shoot as many damn takes as he wants until he gets exactly what he wants, and in his mind, to do otherwise would be a waste. So it goes. Anyway, <em>Mank</em> – <a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/mank-review/\">which is very good</a> – will arrive on Netflix <strong>December 4</strong> following a limited theatrical release starting<strong> November 13</strong>, so soon you&#8217;ll be able to see how all those many Fincher takes worked out.</p>\n<p>The post <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/david-fincher-mank-takes/\">&#8216;Mank&#8217;: It&#8217;s Once Again Time to Point Out David Fincher Shoots Many Takes</a> appeared first on <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com\">/Film</a>.</p>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a href=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/slashfilm?a=oXXhHrXf6pA:2P6SNl0iLBA:yIl2AUoC8zA\"><img 12:49:35","updatedTime":"2020-11-12 12:49:35","title":"‘Peacemaker’ Cast: James Gunn’s ‘The Suicide Squad’ Spin-Off Adds Robert Patrick, Danielle Brooks, and More","link":"https://www.slashfilm.com/?p=645374","description":"<p><img class=\"size-medium wp-image-643696 aligncenter\" src=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/peacemaker-e1604067342820-700x320.jpg\" alt=\"peacemaker cast\" width=\"700\" height=\"320\" srcset=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/peacemaker-e1604067342820-700x320.jpg 700w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/peacemaker-e1604067342820-360x165.jpg 360w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/peacemaker-e1604067342820-768x351.jpg 768w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/peacemaker-e1604067342820.jpg 1200w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px\" /></p>\n<p><strong>James Gunn</strong>&#8216;s <em><strong>Peacemaker</strong></em> cast just got a little bigger.</p>\n<p>The writer/director&#8217;s forthcoming HBO Max series is a spin-off of his upcoming <em>The Suicide Squad</em> movie, which centers on the title character played by John Cena. Now, <strong>Danielle Brooks</strong> (<em>Orange is the New Black</em>), <strong>Robert Patrick</strong> (<em>Terminator 2: Judgment Day</em>), <strong>Jennifer Holland</strong> (<em>Brightburn</em>), and <strong>Chris Conrad</strong> (<em>Patriot</em>) have all been added to the cast. Learn more about their characters below.</p>\n<p><span id=\"more-645374\"></span></p>\n<p>We already knew that Cena and actor Steve Agee would be returning to play their <em>The Suicide Squad</em> characters – Peacemaker and John Economos, respectively – in this new show. But now we have a few details about these new additions.</p>\n<p>Brooks, who was absolutely tremendous on <em>Orange is the New Black</em> as Taystee, will star as Leota Adebayo, and Patrick, who has built a career alternating between playing absolute shitheels and grimy heroes, will portray someone named Auggie Smith. As far as we can tell, neither of those characters have ever appeared in a DC Comics film, TV series, or comic book before, so their identities and how they will factor into the action of this spin-off remains a mystery for now.</p>\n<p>Meanwhile, Holland, who has appeared on <em>American Horror Story</em> and <em>Sun Records</em>, will be reprising her role of Emilia Harcourt from <em>The Suicide Squad</em> film. You may recognize Chris Conrad from his role on <em>Perpetual Grace, LTD.</em>, or, perhaps more likely, as the slick American actor/fighter Johnny Cage from <em>Mortal Kombat: Annihilation</em>. He will play a character named Vigilante/Adrian Chase in this series, a former district attorney whose family is murdered and who dons a costume to punish criminals that the justice system let slip through the cracks.</p>\n<p>Gunn is writing all eight episodes and directing multiple episodes of the show, and he&#8217;s executive producing with <strong>Peter Safran</strong> (<em>Aquaman</em>). Official details about <em>Peacemaker</em> are still being kept under wraps, but we do know that the series will extend the universe that Gunn is creating in <em>The Suicide Squad</em> and that it will specifically &#8220;explore the origins of the character that Cena will play in the upcoming film, a man who believes in peace at any cost — no matter how many people he has to kill to get it.&#8221;</p>\n<p><em>The Suicide Squad</em> is slated to arrive in theaters on <strong>August 6</strong>, <strong>2021</strong>, and <em>Peacemaker</em> does not have a premiere date in place yet.</p>\n<p>The post <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/peacemaker-cast-robert-patrick-danielle-brooks/\">&#8216;Peacemaker&#8217; Cast: James Gunn&#8217;s &#8216;The Suicide Squad&#8217; Spin-Off Adds Robert Patrick, Danielle Brooks, and More</a> appeared first on <a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com\">/Film</a>.</p>\n<div class=\"feedflare\">\n<a width=\"1\" alt=\"\"/>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Wed, 11 Nov 2020 19:30:54 +0000","feedId":21,"bgimg":"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/peacemaker-e1604067342820-700x320.jpg","linkMd5":"c432ec875a4cbfe90e42b4d35b4c02c7","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn21@2020_4/2020/11/12/04-49-37-346_226a8e67ee5c6830.webp","destWidth":700,"destHeight":320,"sourceBytes":33474,"destBytes":21830,"author":"Ben Pearson","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/peacemaker-e1604067342820-700x320.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn21@2020_4/2020/11/12/04-49-37-346_226a8e67ee5c6830.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/slashfilm?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn23@2020_1/2020/11/12/04-50-25-710_9a442e8760d02cd4.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/slashfilm?i=hXbiT5hXWEg:_683D0HZgYo:V_sGLiPBpWU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn77@2020_6/2020/11/12/04-50-27-119_6306d51a81526a46.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/slashfilm?d=I9og5sOYxJI":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn81@2020_5/2020/11/12/04-50-27-215_879c5505f034c3d2.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/slashfilm?i=hXbiT5hXWEg:_683D0HZgYo:EGzC5_Ws_ZE":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn22@2020_6/2020/11/12/04-50-26-760_4cbcc013a0bc8374.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/slashfilm/~4/hXbiT5hXWEg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn49@2020_6/2020/11/12/04-50-26-431_aa39ac4e850a54cb.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1605156575080},{"createdTime":"2020-11-12 12:49:35","updatedTime":"2020-11-12 12:49:35","title":"Decades Ago, ‘MASH’ Fought for the Soul of America – Its Battle Continues Today","link":"https://www.slashfilm.com/?p=644839","description":"<p><img class=\"size-medium wp-image-644851 aligncenter\" src=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/MASH-700x300.jpeg\" alt=\"\" width=\"700\" height=\"300\" srcset=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/MASH.jpeg 700w, https://www.slashfilm.com/wp/wp-content/images/MASH-360x154.jpeg 360w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 700px) 100vw, 700px\" /></p>\n<p><em>(Welcome to <strong><a href=\"https://www.slashfilm.com/category/features/the-soapbox/\">The Soapbox</a></strong>, the space where we get loud, feisty, political, and opinionated about anything and everything.)</em></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Hearing the gentle opening notes of Johnny Mandel’s folk anthem &#8220;</span><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Suicide is Painless&#8221; </span><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">transports a person back to the Korean War and the combat medics of the 4077th.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">As the pandemic crushes spirits and keeps people home longer and longer thanks to the bungled response of our elected leaders, I sought somewhere—anywhere—to be. And the place I landed was </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"><strong>M*A*S*H</strong>. </span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">The show has been part of the cultural consciousness for so long, its ubiquity is taken for granted. I know I took it for granted, at least. It was just always around. I’d watched episodes as a child with my grandfather, who watched it whenever it was on TV. By the time I was aware of it, it was long over, but the reruns are still going strong.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">It felt like a comfortable place to revisit chronologically, with the eyes of an adult and the discerning gaze of a critic. </span><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">And what I found was more incredible than I remembered.</span></p>\n<p><span id=\"more-644839\"></span></p>\n<h2><b>Suicide is Painless</b></h2>\n<p><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">M*A*S*H</span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> began its life as a novel, an absurdist memoir of a combat surgeon forced to cope on the frontlines. There was nothing inherently anti-war about the book. It was later reported that the author, Richard Hornberger, writing as Richard Hooker, did not appreciate the progressive commentary that made the adaptations of his work so daring for their time.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">For its part, the television show is blatantly anti-war. The 1970 Robert Altman film is more nihilistic in its approach, commenting little about the idea of war and instead playing into the absurd comedy of the situations in a subdued fashion. </span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">In every iteration of <em>M*A*S*H</em>, we’re introduced to a man named Hawkeye Pierce. A surgeon and a drunk. A womanizer. And someone who cares about saving people’s lives. </span><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">That’s about where the similarities end and the Hawkeye of the television series, played by Alan Alda, becomes the beating heart of the show and its message.</span></p>\n<h2><b>Biting Social Commentary and Incredible Filmmaking</b></h2>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">The first thing I noticed about </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">M*A*S*H’s </span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">television iteration when I revisited it as an adult was how surprised I was by the sharp writing and it’s incredibly progressive stances. </span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Larry Gelbart, the man who developed </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">M*A*S*H </span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">for television, honed in on the things that made Robert Altman’s film work and turned them up to eleven. One of those things was the social commentary. Another was the relentless march of casualties entering the camp. In the film, the surgery scenes are few compared to the rest of the running time. We might have spent as much time on a football field as the OR in the movie. But Gelbart guided us into surgery and allowed the doctors to show their true colors.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Social issues always took a front seat on the show for its duration. I lost count of the episodes where Hawkeye Pierce visibly shudders when another character uses a racial slur. He always takes the time to correct these people, confidently and matter-of-factly. These are people, not the slurs meant to degrade them. He models the behavior we should all have when calling out racism and bigotry.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Sometimes the surgeons of the 4077th are on the wrong side of history, too. For all of its progress in some areas, the blatant sexism on the show drags it down at times. Margaret Houlihan is referred to as “Hot Lips” excessively, especially early in the show, and her treatment as head nurse is usually the butt of a joke. To the credit of the show and to Loretta Switt who played Margaret, they were able to let her stand her ground and grow into a woman who was strong on her own rather than creating punchlines at her expense. She became less and less defined by the man she was with, whether that was Frank Burns or Donald Penobscott, and became defined by her own interests and aspirations.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">But they also had the forethought to have the characters go too far in their quest to save lives. One specific episode comes to mind where Hawkeye opts to perform an unnecessary surgery on a commanding officer to keep him from mounting a suicide charge and risk the lives of his soldiers. Is it hypocritical of Hawkeye to fail his Hippocratic oath to do no harm by removing a healthy appendix if that saves lives? He can save the lives of the soldiers from the sausage grinder with one simple action. Was he wrong? We’re left to decide for ourselves.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">The show asked difficult questions and successfully blended the comedy and the drama into one seamless, bittersweet and maudlin package. It set the pattern for what a mix of drama and comedy on television could be.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">With a one-camera set up, they were able to capture </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">M*A*S*H </span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">like a film and were free of the locked-down nature of a three-camera sitcom. The one downside of the show is the producers weren’t able to escape the dreaded laugh track and, if you’re in the United States, it still appears on Hulu. If someone from Hulu is reading, please give us the non-laugh track versions of the show they used in the UK, who rejected the offensive laugh track out of hand.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">The show also broke ground with episodes that look brilliant in retrospect and are so well-copied that we think nothing of them anymore. Killing Col. Henry Blake (MacLean Stevenson) after he’d been sent home, was a masterstroke. In the rightfully famous episode, Radar O’Reilly (Gary Burghoff) comes in and somberly lets the doctors of the OR know the news, devastating both Blake’s friends and the audience as well.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Another landmark episode was “The Interview,” the last that Gelbart delivered before moving on to other things. In it, they had the cast interview with a real news reporter in-character. The mix of sharp scripted responses and improv created one of the most compelling half-hours of television I’ve ever seen. </span></p>\n<p><iframe width=\"740\" height=\"416\" src=\"https://www.youtube.com/embed/_WopzVVFXyM?feature=oembed\" frameborder=\"0\" allow=\"accelerometer; autoplay; clipboard-write; encrypted-media; gyroscope; picture-in-picture\" allowfullscreen></iframe></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">These episodes set the template of how you could stretch the narrative of a television show and we now see these stretches all the time. There’s a lot left to study about </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">M*A*S*H </span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">and we’ll be seeing shows emulating it for the foreseeable future.</span></p>\n<h2><b>Flag-Waving Buffoons</b></h2>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">One aspect of the show that feels relevant today is Frank Burns (Larry Linville). Burns is a flag-waving buffoon of the highest order. He is a dyed-in-the-wool conservative and hates everything about the progress made by Franklin Roosevelt. He treats the locals in Korea as though they’re animals and he believes whole-heartedly in the war and the American correctness of it all. He paints himself as a true patriot and feels like a completely relevant caricature of the jingoistic Trump supporters we see in our world today. Like Burns, they would rather wave flags and proclaim their own importance than have an ounce of empathy or learn what the right thing to do might be. Burns is an authoritarian, and we see the dangers of letting an authoritarian get command every time Burns is left in charge of the camp. </span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">It wasn’t that </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">M*A*S*H </span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">was somehow more prescient, but it was better at reading the room. Those authoritarian and jingoistic impulses </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">lead </span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">to fascism. They always have. They are currently. It never ends well. </span><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Revisiting the series and seeing Burns brilliantly played as a complete buffoon no one takes seriously was a perfect antidote to the things I still see on the news today.</span></p>\n<h2><b>Hawkeye Today</b></h2>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Hawkweye Pierce, played brilliantly by Alan Alda, is the beating heart of the show and its most important moral compass. His compassion and righteous fury added as much drama as the alleged comedy could bear. Alda threads the needle between the poles of comedy and drama, giving the show a gravitas that could sneak up on you if you weren’t careful.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Alda was the mouthpiece for the progressive vision of the show, and he found that important before he even agreed to take the part. <a href=\"https://www.forbes.com/sites/janelevere/2013/10/31/alan-alda-reminisces-about-his-star-turn-as-hawkeye-in-mash/\">In an interview with Forbes in 2013</a>, he said that his “heart was always against any war,” and explained that the only way </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">M*A*S*H</span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> “could make a point about war was to be truthful about the Korean War.”</span><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> And truthful they were. </span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">For 11 years as the show ran, they told many pointed stories about the war. When the show started, the United States was embroiled in Vietnam, and having such popular anti-war programming about a war couldn’t have hurt the case for peace in that conflict.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">For Alda’s part, he never stopped being a moral compass for us, the impassioned viewer. It’s no surprise that Alan Alda ended up becoming a creative consultant on the show and won Emmys for writing and directing as well as acting. He inhabited the role and brought those lessons to the world for the 11 years </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">M*A*S*H </span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">was on the air.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Even still, he persists.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">“Almost 63 million people voted for Donald Trump in 2016, but in 1983, more than 106 million people watched the last episode of </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">M*A*S*H.</span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\"> So, it seems that by this president’s standard, I’m a bigger deal than he is,” Alda <a href=\"https://www.washingtonpost.com/opinions/2020/10/29/alan-alda-vote-for-science-against-trump/?utm_source=reddit.com\">wrote in the Washington Post last month</a>. </span><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">He brought back the voice of the show and the morals it espoused to plead with the world to vote against Donald Trump. </span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">“Trump once said he could shoot someone on Fifth Avenue without consequences. At this moment, we are all on Fifth Avenue,” he wrote.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Thankfully, people listened.</span></p>\n<h2><b>Back to the Swamp</b></h2>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">If episodes of </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">M*A*S*H </span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">were to air today, no doubt the network would be getting letters from angry Trump supporters asking why they allowed snowflakes and SJWs to be put in charge of a show set during the Korean War that featured soldiers and doctors and combat. They wouldn’t like having a finger wagged in their face about how wrong the concept of war was. And they certainly wouldn’t like the sacred lambs of capitalism and war profiteering and geopolitics being made the butt of jokes. And they would absolutely not love to see themselves represented by characters like Frank Burns and Charles Emerson Winchester III (David Ogden Stiers), as they were usually the butt of the joke, too.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">But those worldviews </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">are </span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">jokes. </span><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">And </span><i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">M*A*S*H </span></i><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">showed us how to laugh at them.</span></p>\n<p><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">Go back and revisit the series. It’s streaming on Hulu. You’ll find it relevant, hilarious, and heartbreaking in equal measure. </span><span style=\"font-weight: 400;\">It’s just what the surgeon who sounds like Groucho Marx ordered.</span></p>\n<p>The
